<div id="review_2435729148" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2435729148" />
    <a title="Philautia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42737452-philautia"><img alt="Philautia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605016502p2/42737452.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2435729148?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philautia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philautia" href="/user/show/42737452-philautia">Philautia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/31116136-os-maias">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42737452-philautia?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42737452-philautia?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42737452-philautia?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42737452-philautia?shelf=portuguese">portuguese</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2435729148&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2435729148" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer997248649863534014">I can't believe I finally finished reading this. It took me a while! I felt like I was going to be reading this book forever. One of my daily chores was to read ten pages a day, and now I don't have to do that, which is weird. I won't have to read it super quickly when it's time for me to study it at school! I'll just have to reread the most important parts! Yay me, getting things done before they're due!<br/><br/>Anyway, let's move on to my actual thoughts and opinions...<br/><br/>First of all, I would like to ann</span>
  <span id="freeText997248649863534014" style="display:none">I can't believe I finally finished reading this. It took me a while! I felt like I was going to be reading this book forever. One of my daily chores was to read ten pages a day, and now I don't have to do that, which is weird. I won't have to read it super quickly when it's time for me to study it at school! I'll just have to reread the most important parts! Yay me, getting things done before they're due!<br /><br />Anyway, let's move on to my actual thoughts and opinions...<br /><br />First of all, I would like to announce this to the world: the second chapter? AMAZING. I was absolutely captivated by the story of Pedro da Maia and I'm sad that it didn't last longer. His story was so tragic, and Afonso da Maia went through so much. The poor dude had to go through his wife's death, followed by disowning his son because he sort of... abandoned him and married the woman who eventually ran away with another man (and took his daughter!). Then his son comes back and kills himself on the same night. YIKES, OLD MAN.<br /><br />Second, I immediately removed a star because of what we in Portugal call <i>"palha"</i>. So much <i>palha</i>. <i>Palha</i> is basically filler - those parts that are completely unnecessary to the plot. Because of that, I felt like the pacing of the novel was all over the place. The first chapter featured a LONG-ASS DESCRIPTION OF THE HOUSE THE MAIAS CAME TO INHABIT IN THE FALL OF 1875, along with Afonso's story. So that was a weird chapter. The second chapter is packed with the complete story of Pedro da Maia. Then, for the next 300 pages we get to see Carlos and Co. and their shenanigans, I guess. That chunk of pages between Pedro's death and Carlos meeting Maria Eduarda felt like it took forever and a lot of it felt unnecessary to the plot. I felt like the book could tell its story in 400 pages, if you removed all of the filler. So much of it was boring and such a chore to get through.<br /><br />Let's talk about characters! These characters are, for the most part, both likeable and unlikeable at the same time. You see, you like them because they're trainwrecks, and you might even find that endearing, just like I did! Then one of them says something that makes you want to strangle them.<br /><br />My favourite character at the start was Afonso, because he's the nice grandpa. Who doesn't like a nice chill grandpa? Then Ega got introduced, and to quote myself: "Ega is a bisexual icon and nothing can convince me otherwise. thanks for coming to my ted talk" (nothing really implies that he's bi, just a <s>bibe</s> vibe.) However, Ega is kind of a dumbass and his opinions are mostly a) bullshit that he doesn't really believe in or b) bullshit that you wish he didn't believe in. In the end, I don't hate him, but I don't love him like I used to either.<br /><br />So now, at the end, I think I can say for sure that my favourite character is Cruges! I don't even know his first name, but oh well, who cares? Cruges just likes to play his piano. Let me tell you the story that made me love him. Cruges, Alencar and Carlos went to Sintra, and Cruges really wanted some <i>queijadas</i> (pastries!) and kept reminding himself "Don't forget the <i>queijadas</i>...". What happens next, you may ask? HE FORGOT THE FREAKING <i>QUEIJADAS</i>. On page 256, they're going back home and he just screams and says "I FORGOT THE <i>QUEIJADAS</i>!" and me, being an absolute trainwreck, I could relate. <br /><br />Carlos and Maria Eduarda meet on page 364 and the pacing picks up. You see, Maria is his long lost sister, but they don't know that, so they start seeing each other. And kissing. And having sexy-times. At one point, they're planning on marrying each other, which would be very YIKES.<br /><br />Fortunately (or unfortunately, because sometimes ignorance is bliss, depends on your point of view), Ega finds out the truth and tells Carlos and they split up. <br /><br />Here's the thing: I DID NOT EXPECT TO CRY WHILE READING A MANDATORY READ. Actually, scratch that, when I found out that Afonso dies, I expected to maybe shed a tear or two when it happened. I DID NOT EXPECT TO SOB ON THE FLOOR OF MY ROOM. The descriptions were fantastic and heartbreaking, and I have a soft spot for grandparents! I love all four of mine and seeing Carlos absolutely devastated at his grandfather's passing was enough to leave me devastated. Especially because he blamed himself. <br /><br />Why did he blame himself? Yikes, my guy/gal/pal. Carlos actually shared the bed with his sister one last time while being aware of the truth and his grandpa found out. I WOULD DIE TOO. He also had a heart disease, so there's that. <br /><br />TL;DR: Liked it, had some issues with it, don't know how to conclude reviews but the book is good!</span>
  <a data-text-id="997248649863534014" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119427644" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119427644?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luciana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luciana" href="/user/show/4219843-luciana">Luciana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4219843-luciana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4219843-luciana?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4219843-luciana?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4219843-luciana?shelf=portuguese">portuguese</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119427644" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14225910141394055700">This was the second time I read this book, and it felt just as good as in the first time. Yes, it's slow paced. Yes, it's about the upper classes, and I truly don't understand why this is sometimes a complaint. Eça de Queirós, as it is to be expected from the author who introduced Realism in Portugal, is extremely critical of the society he writes about. In his books, if anything looks too perfect to be true, well, it probably is. Nothing is sacred: romantism, religion, position, money, politics</span>
  <span id="freeText14225910141394055700" style="display:none">This was the second time I read this book, and it felt just as good as in the first time. Yes, it's slow paced. Yes, it's about the upper classes, and I truly don't understand why this is sometimes a complaint. Eça de Queirós, as it is to be expected from the author who introduced Realism in Portugal, is extremely critical of the society he writes about. In his books, if anything looks too perfect to be true, well, it probably is. Nothing is sacred: romantism, religion, position, money, politics, adultery, family. Everything is talked about, everything is subject to his criticism, not without great humor though.<br /><br />Pedro da Maia is a fervently religious gentleman who falls in love with Maria Monforte, a woman considered to be beneath him in every possible way by the current society. Pedro's father is therefore firmly opposed to the marriage. Pedro, who's hopelessly and almost blindly in love, defies his father's authority and marries her anyway. The consequences of this single act will prove to be disastrous. This book, however, is not so much Pedro's story, but also his father's, and especially his son's. It is both a coming of age story and one about the loss of innocence. And the characters are so alive and so human. From the handsome young gentleman Carlos Eduardo to the achingly beautiful Maria, the wild and intelligent João da Ega, the repulsive Damaso, they are all so richly described and vivid. But my favorite was probably the old Afonso, who had to go through so much and stayed strong for so long.<br /><br />Eça de Queirós is sometimes compared to the likes of Tolstoy, Balzac and Dickens. Indeed, had he been English, French or Russian, this book would probably be as widely known as Anna Karenina or other classics of the genre. I cannot recommend it enough to anyone who loves literature from the 19th century. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14225910141394055700" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5499733276530300383">I absolutely loved this book. I have read it before when I was 17 and taking my first steps into Portuguese literature, as this was one of those recommended/mandatory readings.<br/>I have decided to reread some specific parts to draw some inspiration from Eça's amazing description skills but I just couldn't help myself. This book is brilliantly written! And despite his length and its reputation of being 'boring' or 'too descriptive' (which I highly dispute), I got so caught up in the story (again!) t</span>
  <span id="freeText5499733276530300383" style="display:none">I absolutely loved this book. I have read it before when I was 17 and taking my first steps into Portuguese literature, as this was one of those recommended/mandatory readings.<br />I have decided to reread some specific parts to draw some inspiration from Eça's amazing description skills but I just couldn't help myself. This book is brilliantly written! And despite his length and its reputation of being 'boring' or 'too descriptive' (which I highly dispute), I got so caught up in the story (again!) that I just had to read it (again!)... :P<br />Ega was (back then) and still remains my favorite character. Nonetheless, I must admit that I have looked into Mara Eduarda and Carlos love story wth different eyes. I really have to admit that age hugely influences the way we take in the stories and I'm starting to think if our students at the age of 16 are truly prepared to entirely grasp the concept behind this book.<br />It is, behind any doubt, even the slightest tiny shadow of a doubt, one of the masterpieces of Portuguese Literature, and one everyone should read!<br />I laughed with Ega's sharpness and vision of life (we all know that deep down he truly cares and gives a cr*p about his surroundings) and the depth in his character... top fav character! =D<br />I was frustrated and annoyed by Damaso's actions and I got lost in so many feelings (love, anxiety, angst and sorrow) towards Carlos and Eduarda. That scene at the dining table when he shows he has them in the right place.... ahhhhh! :P <br />I'm really struggling here to not give away any spoilers! <br />Just read it... Do it! :P </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3389751726" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer251221992980606340">Depicts a rapidly changing Portugal and society and customs of the upper classes during the late 19th Century. It's pitting Romanticism against Modernism, Science versus Religion, and many other beliefs that had held to be true in the past against this changing tide of the future. This is all seen through the lens of the Maias, a prominent family in Lisbon. Alfonso Maia, the patriarch of the family and grounded in tradition sees changes in his son and grandson who are embroiled in relationships </span>
  <span id="freeText251221992980606340" style="display:none">Depicts a rapidly changing Portugal and society and customs of the upper classes during the late 19th Century. It's pitting Romanticism against Modernism, Science versus Religion, and many other beliefs that had held to be true in the past against this changing tide of the future. This is all seen through the lens of the Maias, a prominent family in Lisbon. Alfonso Maia, the patriarch of the family and grounded in tradition sees changes in his son and grandson who are embroiled in relationships that cause Pedro(son)to commit suicide and where the grandson becomes involved with his sister(unknowingly)as they pursue more modernistic lives. Portugal is changing into a positivist culture, coming into conflict with an older generation that must come to accept or at least learn to live in a changing world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="251221992980606340" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_14813635" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14813635" />
    <a title="Mariana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/882507-mariana"><img alt="Mariana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458767787p2/882507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14813635?book_show_action=true">Feb 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariana" href="/user/show/882507-mariana">Mariana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/2268171.Os_Maias">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who likes social criticism in particular, and a great, wit-filled story in general.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/882507-mariana?shelf=read-me-senseless-books">read-me-senseless-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/882507-mariana?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/882507-mariana?shelf=portuguese-literature">portuguese-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/882507-mariana?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14813635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15332318231792767477">Welcome to Portuguese 19th century society! Sit back and enjoy a throughly well written, critical, satirical, humoristic and surprisingly actual view of its virtues and flaws, its characters and habits, its morals and ideas - all by the hand (or should I say pen?) of the most extraordinary of Portuguese novelists and Realistic writers.<br/><br/><i>The Maias</i> tells the story of a family - from Afonso da Maia (the great patriarch) to his son Pedro (who loves greatly and tragically) to Pedro's son, Carlos da Mai</span>
  <span id="freeText15332318231792767477" style="display:none">Welcome to Portuguese 19th century society! Sit back and enjoy a throughly well written, critical, satirical, humoristic and surprisingly actual view of its virtues and flaws, its characters and habits, its morals and ideas - all by the hand (or should I say pen?) of the most extraordinary of Portuguese novelists and Realistic writers.<br /><br /><i>The Maias</i> tells the story of a family - from Afonso da Maia (the great patriarch) to his son Pedro (who loves greatly and tragically) to Pedro's son, Carlos da Maia, the main character, a <i>dandy</i> and a newly licensed doctor - who all dwell in the family's house in Lisbon - the Ramalhete, or House of the Bouquet. <br /><br />However, <i>The Maias</i> does not revolve only around the Maias themselves - it is full of wonderful characters, representative of the main figures and social classes of the time. From João da Ega, Carlos's eccentric best friend, to Dâmaso Salcede, a <i>nouveau riche</i> full of bad habits; from Tomás de Alencar, an old poet distraught by Naturalism and Realism, to Cohen, a jew and the National Bank director. Many others still penetrate the world of <i>The Maias</i> - like Maria Eduarda, who falls in love with Carlos without knowing the future consequences. <br /><br />It is impossible to sum up <i>The Maias</i> in just a few paragraphs - you'll just have to read and discover the marvellous characters and writing this book encloses. This was the first book I ever read from Eça de Queirós, and the one who made him one of my favourite companions of long-houred, pleasant and laugh-filled readings. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15332318231792767477" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1262548688" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer12439650897707941215">If not for the portuguese lessons' program, I would probably not read this book, at least in the near future, and what a great loss it would be. Although many colleagues and friends, and even my elder sister, told me that "Os Maias" is not an interesting book, specially the beginning, I completely disagree.<br/> Reading throughout the book, I was amazed a lot of times by Eça's writing and how well he described late 19th century's Portugal society with all it's problems and qualities from his point of</span>
  <span id="freeText12439650897707941215" style="display:none">If not for the portuguese lessons' program, I would probably not read this book, at least in the near future, and what a great loss it would be. Although many colleagues and friends, and even my elder sister, told me that "Os Maias" is not an interesting book, specially the beginning, I completely disagree.<br /> Reading throughout the book, I was amazed a lot of times by Eça's writing and how well he described late 19th century's Portugal society with all it's problems and qualities from his point of view, with a lot of aspects that are still present in modern society, more than an hundred years later.<br /> Even if very descritive at some points, "Os Maias" is filled with lots of action and enthusiastic moments like the horse races when the autor passes the exactly feeling of the people that were watching with high expectations, and suddenly, we are transported to this ambient and forget about everything that's going on around us, something that is very rare to happen to me and this book achieved it.<br /> While I loved lots of things about this book, I can't give a perfect score to this masterpiece as I think that sometimes it gets too descritive (I felt it more in some middle chapters than in the very beginning, which most people who have read say it's the worst part), and the fact that no one in the book has any kind of luck or happy life.</span>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText2126148561218784590" style="display:none">a friend keeps recommending jose saramago to me. i don't think i'm smart enough for saramago but saramago calls this dude portugal's greatest novelist - so i think it's a worthy replacement for saramago.<br /><br />the nyt review makes this novel sound like something of a socially conscious bodice-ripper, which appeals to me.<br /><br />UPDATE: i didn't find this book to be terrible socially conscious, but it was conscious (and critical) of Polite Society. there is a definite kinship between wharton's most scathing commentary on new york society and this book's portrayal of the vapid lisbonites. the prose was smooth and beautiful, despite being a translation. it is pretty steamy, though, and i can't help but wonder if nabokov ever had a gander at this before writing Ada...<br /><br />highly recommended.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3909105717411327166">Weighing in at 628 pages, this is a book that begins to hurt one’s pinky when tries to prop it up in bed. But one does not let this stop her.<br/><br/>Published in 1888, The Maias is a sweeping multi-generation stretching saga not unlike that of the Forsytes, but in Portugal. Young men in fine boots discuss art over roast game birds. A woman with pale plump arms wear an ear of corn in her hair at the theater (I’d like to see someone pull that off). A tragic secret seems too obvious to be true, and you are</span>
  <span id="freeText3909105717411327166" style="display:none">Weighing in at 628 pages, this is a book that begins to hurt one’s pinky when tries to prop it up in bed. But one does not let this stop her.<br /><br />Published in 1888, The Maias is a sweeping multi-generation stretching saga not unlike that of the Forsytes, but in Portugal. Young men in fine boots discuss art over roast game birds. A woman with pale plump arms wear an ear of corn in her hair at the theater (I’d like to see someone pull that off). A tragic secret seems too obvious to be true, and you are kept guessing back and forth.<br /><br />Skillful. Fully satisfying. </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Lwo/FEMjb3tqgqMjCgCrrMQWc3/EkQ2IeqhPJ6Z4LkgVLFbG+H2mE82pF0L99aFzEkmUtwocg+8h1gmWazvc0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('k/4K8Gllh5W7O1oX7lCq15AKdszWRJ8hoG/Yph2KYbOp2GMi0jtO/RwQ7nYZpaAIRlWRBBjJEUb7EZ4X0MmTKQ==')})
  }
//]]>

Desde menino, ouvia eu contar a triste história de meu tio paterno Simão António Botelho. Minha tia, irmã dele, solicitada por minha curiosidade, estava sempre pronta a repetir o facto aligado à sua mocidade. Lembrou-me naturalmente, na cadeia, muitas vezes, meu tio, que ali deveria estar inscrito no livro das entradas no cárcere e no das saídas para o degredo. Folheei os livros desde os de 1800, e achei a notícia com pouca fadiga, e alvoroços de contentamento, como se em minha alçada estivesse adornar-lhe a memória como recompensa das suas trágicas e afrontosas dores em vida tão breve. Sabia eu que em casa de minha irmã estavam acantoados uns maços de papéis antigos, tendentes a esclarecer a nebulosa história de meu tio. Pedi aos contemporâneos que o conheceram notícias e miudezas, a fim de entrar de consciência naquele trabalho. Escrevi o romance em quinze dias, os mais atormentados de minha vida. Tão horrorizada tenho deles a memória, que nunca mais abrirei o «Amor de Perdição», nem lhe passarei a lima sobre os defeitos nas edições futuras, se é que não saiu tolhiço incorrigível da primeira. Não sei se lá digo que meu tio Simão chorava, e menos sei se o leitor chorou com ele. De mim lhe juro que…»\nVão passados quase dois anos, depois que protestei não mais abrir este romance. […] Este livro, cujo êxito se me antolhava mau, quando eu o ia escrevendo, teve uma recepção de primazia sobre todos os seus irmãos. Movia-me à desconfiança o ser ele triste, sem interpolação de risos, sombrio, e rematado por catástrofe de confranger o ânimo dos leitores, que se interessam na boa sorte de uns, e no castigo de outros personagens. Em honra e louvor das pessoas que estimaram o meu livro, confessarei agradavelmente que julguei mal delas. […] O livro agradou como está. Seria desacerto e ingratidão demudar sensivelmente, quer na essência, quer na compostura, o que, tal qual é, foi bem recebido.\nDo Prefácio da Segunda Edição. gentil andorinha.\n\nCom a duração de três doces estações, o improvável romance entre as duas criaturas das «profundas do passado quando os bichos falavam» sobrevive às críticas sociais, à diferença       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6913270.E_a_de_Queir_s">About Eça de Queirós</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6913270.E_a_de_Queir_s"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1360063685p3/6913270.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6913270.E_a_de_Queir_s">Eça de Queirós
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
864
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11840377428401791541">José Maria Eça de Queirós was a novelist committed to social reform who introduced Naturalism and Realism to Portugal. He is often considered to be the greatest Portuguese novelist, certainly the leading 19th-century Portuguese novelist whose fame was international. The son of a prominent magistrate, Eça de Queiroz spent his early years with relatives and was sent to boarding school at the age of </span>
  <span id="freeText11840377428401791541" style="display:none">José Maria Eça de Queirós was a novelist committed to social reform who introduced Naturalism and Realism to Portugal. He is often considered to be the greatest Portuguese novelist, certainly the leading 19th-century Portuguese novelist whose fame was international. The son of a prominent magistrate, Eça de Queiroz spent his early years with relatives and was sent to boarding school at the age of five. After receiving his degree in law in 1866 from the University of Coimbra, where he read widely French, he settled in Lisbon. There his father, who had since married Eça de Queiroz' mother, made up for past neglect by helping the young man make a start in the legal profession. Eça de Queiroz' real interest lay in literature, however, and soon his short stories - ironic, fantastic, macabre, and often gratuitously shocking - and essays on a wide variety of subjects began to appear in the "Gazeta de Portugal". By 1871 he had become closely associated with a group of rebellious Portuguese intellectuals committed to social and artistic reform and known as the Generation of '70. Eça de Queiroz gave one of a series of lectures sponsored by the group in which he denounced contemporary Portuguese literature as unoriginal and hypocritical. He served as consul, first in Havana (1872-74), then in England, UK - in Newcastle upon Tyne (1874-79) and in Bristol (1879-88). During this time he wrote the novels for which he is best remembered, attempting to bring about social reform in Portugal through literature by exposing what he held to be the evils and the absurdities of the traditional order. His first novel, "O crime do Padre Amaro" (1875; "The Sin of Father Amaro", 1962), describes the destructive effects of celibacy on a priest of weak character and the dangers of fanaticism in a provincial Portuguese town. A biting satire on the romantic ideal of passion and its tragic consequences appears in his next novel, "O Primo Basílio" (1878; "Cousin Bazilio", 1953). Caustic satire characterizes the novel that is generally considered Eça de Queiroz' masterpiece, "Os Maias (1888; "The Maias", 1965), a detailed depiction of upper middle-class and aristocratic Portuguese society. His last novels are sentimental, unlike his earlier work. "A Cidade e as Serras" (1901; "The City and the Mountains", 1955) extols the beauty of the Portuguese countryside and the joys of rural life. Eça de Queiroz was appointed consul in Paris in 1888, where he served until his death. Of his posthumously published works, "Contos" (1902) is a collection of short stories, and "Últimas Páginas" (1912) includes saints' legends. Translations of his works persisted into the second half of the 20th century.<br /><br />Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0211055/bio">http://www.imdb.com/name/nm0211055/bio</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="11840377428401791541" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1238374-os-maias">Quotes from Os Maias</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;- Falhamos a vida, menino! <br />- Creio que sim... Mas todo o mundo mais ou menos a falha. Isto é, falha-se sempre na realidade aquela vida que se planeou com a imaginação. Diz-se: «vou ser assim, porque a beleza está em ser assim». E nunca se é assim, é-se invariavelmente assado, como dizia o pobre marquês. Ás vezes melhor, mas sempre diferente.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/495410---falhamos-a-vida-menino---creio-que-sim-mas">117 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;É extraordinário! Neste abençoado país todos os políticos têm «imenso talento». A oposição confessa sempre que os ministros, que ela cobre de injúrias, tem, à parte os disparates que fazem, um «talento de primeira ordem»! Por outro lado a maioria admite que a oposição, a quem ela contantemente recrimina pelos disparates que fez, está cheia de «robustíssimos talentos»! De resto todo o mundo concorda que o país é uma choldra. E resulta portanto este facto supracómico: um país governado «com imenso talento», que é de todos na Europa, segundo o consenso unânime, o mais estùpidamente governado! Eu proponho isto, a ver: que, como os talentos sempre falham, se experimentem uma vez os imbecis!&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/242786-extraordin-rio-neste-aben-oado-pa-s-todos-os-pol-ticos-t-m-imenso">62 likes</a>
